<p><b>John Calvin Coolidge, Jr.</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'">uː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/dʒ/ 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; July 4, 1872 – January 5, 1933) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">30th President of the United States</a> (1923–29). A <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> lawyer from <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, Coolidge worked his way up the ladder of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> state <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, eventually becoming <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Massachusetts" title="Governor of Massachusetts">governor</a> of that state. His response to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Police_Strike" title="Boston Police Strike">Boston Police Strike</a> of 1919 thrust him into the national spotlight and gave him a reputation as a man of decisive action. Soon after, he was elected as the 29th <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">vice president</a> in 1920 and succeeded to the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">presidency</a> upon the sudden death of <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> in 1923. Elected in his own right in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1924" title="United States presidential election, 1924">1924</a>, he gained a reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Small_government" title="Small government">small-government</a> <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States#Early_20th_Century" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a>, and also as a man who said very little, although having a rather dry sense of humor.</p>
<p>Coolidge restored public confidence in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding#Scandals" title="Warren G. Harding">scandals</a> of his predecessor's administration, and left office with considerable popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967420.E2.80.9321Greenberg200649.E2.80.9353_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967420.E2.80.9321Greenberg200649.E2.80.9353-1">[1]</a></sup> As a Coolidge biographer wrote, "He embodied the spirit and hopes of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a>, could interpret their longings and express their opinions. That he did represent the genius of the average is the most convincing proof of his strength."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940500_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940500-2">[2]</a></sup> Coolidge's retirement was relatively short, as he died at the age of 60 in January 1933, less than two months before his immediate successor, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, left office.</p>
<p>Though his reputation underwent a renaissance during the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States#General_findings" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">modern assessments of Coolidge's presidency</a> are divided. He is adulated among advocates of smaller government and <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i>; supporters of an active central government generally view him less favorably, while both sides praise his stalwart support of racial equality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199812.E2.80.9313Greenberg20061.E2.80.937_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199812.E2.80.9313Greenberg20061.E2.80.937-3">[3]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_family_history">Birth and family history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Birth and family history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CoolidgeAmherst.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/CoolidgeAmherst.png/170px-CoolidgeAmherst.png" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/CoolidgeAmherst.png/255px-CoolidgeAmherst.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/CoolidgeAmherst.png/340px-CoolidgeAmherst.png 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="524" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CoolidgeAmherst.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge as an Amherst College undergraduate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>John Calvin Coolidge, Jr. was born in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth,_Vermont" title="Plymouth, Vermont">Plymouth Notch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor_County,_Vermont" title="Windsor County, Vermont">Windsor County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, on July 4, 1872, the only U.S. president to be born on <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a>. He was the elder of the two children of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin_Coolidge_Sr." title="John Calvin Coolidge Sr.">John Calvin Coolidge Sr.</a> (1845–1926) and Victoria Josephine Moor (1846–85). Coolidge Senior engaged in many occupations, but developed a statewide reputation as a prosperous farmer, storekeeper and public servant. He held various local offices, including <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace" title="Justice of the peace">justice of the peace</a> and tax collector and served in the Vermont House of Representatives as well as the Vermont Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199822_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199822-4">[4]</a></sup> Coolidge's mother was the daughter of a Plymouth Notch farmer. She was chronically ill and died, perhaps from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, when Coolidge was twelve years old. His younger sister, Abigail Grace Coolidge (1875–1890), died at the age of fifteen, probably of appendicitis, when Coolidge was eighteen. Coolidge's father remarried in 1891, to a schoolteacher, and lived to the age of eighty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194017McCoy19675White193811_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194017McCoy19675White193811-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's family had deep roots in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>; his earliest American ancestor, John Coolidge, emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Cottenham" title="Cottenham">Cottenham</a>, Cambridgeshire, England, around 1630 and settled in <a href="/wiki/Watertown,_Massachusetts" title="Watertown, Massachusetts">Watertown, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194012_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194012-6">[6]</a></sup> Another ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Rice_(1638)" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund Rice (1638)">Edmund Rice</a>, arrived at Watertown in 1638. Coolidge's great-great-grandfather, also named John Coolidge, was an American military officer in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a> and one of the first <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Selectmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Board of Selectmen">selectmen</a> of the town of Plymouth Notch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess19407_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess19407-7">[7]</a></sup> His grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Galusha_Coolidge" title="Calvin Galusha Coolidge">Calvin Galusha Coolidge</a>, served in the Vermont House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199824_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199824-8">[8]</a></sup> Many of Coolidge's ancestors were farmers, and numerous distant cousins were prominent in politics.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_and_marriage">Early career and marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early career and marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prof._Charles_E._Garman_-_Amherst_College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Prof._Charles_E._Garman_-_Amherst_College.jpg/170px-Prof._Charles_E._Garman_-_Amherst_College.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3c/Prof._Charles_E._Garman_-_Amherst_College.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="233" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prof._Charles_E._Garman_-_Amherst_College.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prof. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Garman" title="Charles Edward Garman">Charles Edward Garman</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_law_practice">Education and law practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education and law practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge attended <a href="/wiki/Black_River_Academy" title="Black River Academy">Black River Academy</a> and then <a href="/wiki/St._Johnsbury_Academy" title="St. Johnsbury Academy">St. Johnsbury Academy</a>, before enrolling at <a href="/wiki/Amherst_College" title="Amherst College">Amherst College</a>, where he distinguished himself in the debating class, as a senior joined the fraternity <a href="/wiki/Phi_Gamma_Delta" title="Phi Gamma Delta">Phi Gamma Delta</a> and graduated <i>cum laude</i>. While there, Coolidge was profoundly influenced by philosophy professor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Garman" title="Charles Edward Garman">Charles Edward Garman</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> mystic, with a <a href="/wiki/Neo-Hegelianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Hegelianism">neo-Hegelian</a> philosophy.</p>
<p>Coolidge explained Garman's ethics forty years later,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>there is a standard of righteousness that might does not make right, that the end does not justify the means, and that expediency as a working principle is bound to fail. The only hope of perfecting human relationships is in accordance with the law of service under which men are not so solicitous about what they shall get as they are about what they shall give. Yet people are entitled to the rewards of their industry. What they earn is theirs, no matter how small or how great. But the possession of property carries the obligation to use it in a larger service...<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite193843.E2.80.9344_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite193843.E2.80.9344-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At his father's urging after graduation, Coolidge moved to <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton, Massachusetts</a> to become a lawyer. To avoid the cost of <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law school</a>, Coolidge followed the common practice of apprenticing with a local law firm, Hammond &amp; Field, and <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">reading law</a> with them. <a href="/wiki/John_C_Hammond" title="John C Hammond">John C. Hammond</a> and Henry P. Field, both Amherst graduates, introduced Coolidge to law practice in the county seat of <a href="/wiki/Hampshire_County,_Massachusetts" title="Hampshire County, Massachusetts">Hampshire County</a>. In 1897, Coolidge was admitted to the bar, becoming a <a href="/wiki/Country_lawyer" title="Country lawyer">country lawyer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlaes201366.E2.80.9368_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlaes201366.E2.80.9368-10">[10]</a></sup> With his savings and a small inheritance from his grandfather, Coolidge opened his own law office in Northampton in 1898. He practiced commercial law, believing that he served his clients best by staying out of court. As his reputation as a hard-working and diligent attorney grew, local banks and other businesses began to retain his services.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194074.E2.80.9381McCoy196722.E2.80.9326_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194074.E2.80.9381McCoy196722.E2.80.9326-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Coolidge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Grace_Coolidge.jpg/170px-Grace_Coolidge.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Grace_Coolidge.jpg/255px-Grace_Coolidge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Grace_Coolidge.jpg/340px-Grace_Coolidge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3893" data-file-height="4896" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Coolidge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Grace_Coolidge" title="Grace Coolidge">Grace Coolidge</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1905, Coolidge met <a href="/wiki/Grace_Coolidge" title="Grace Coolidge">Grace Anna Goodhue</a>, a University of Vermont graduate and teacher at the <a href="/wiki/Clarke_School_for_the_Deaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarke School for the Deaf">Clarke School for the Deaf</a>. That year they were engaged in early summer and married on October 4, 1905 at 2:30&#160;p.m. at Grace's family's house: in the front of the bay window of the parlor, after an attempt in vain by Grace's mother to postpone the vows: she was never enamored with Coolidge, nor he with her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryson2013187_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryson2013187-12">[12]</a></sup> The newlyweds went on honeymoon to Montreal, originally planned for two weeks but cut short by a week at Coolidge's request. After 25 years he wrote of Grace, "for almost a quarter of a century she has borne with my infirmities and I have rejoiced in her graces".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite193861_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite193861-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Coolidges had two sons: <a href="/wiki/John_Coolidge" title="John Coolidge">John</a> (September 7, 1906 – May 31, 2000) and Calvin, Jr. (April 13, 1908 – July 7, 1924). Calvin's death at age 16 from blood poisoning brought on by an infected blister "hurt him terribly," according to son John. John became a railroad executive, helped to start the Coolidge Foundation, and was instrumental in creating the <a href="/wiki/President_Calvin_Coolidge_State_Historic_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="President Calvin Coolidge State Historic Site">President Calvin Coolidge State Historic Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnNYTobit_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnNYTobit-14">[14]</a></sup> Coolidge was frugal, and when it came to securing a home, he insisted upon renting. Henry Field had a pew in Edwards <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> Church, and Grace was a member, but Coolidge never formally joined the congregation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlaes201391_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlaes201391-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_political_office">Local political office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Local political office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="City_offices">City offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: City offices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> was dominant in New England in Coolidge's time, and he followed Hammond's and Field's example by becoming active in local politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199849.E2.80.9351_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199849.E2.80.9351-16">[16]</a></sup> In 1896, Coolidge campaigned for Republican presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>, and the next year he was selected to be a member of the Republican City Committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite193851.E2.80.9353_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite193851.E2.80.9353-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1898, he won election to the <a href="/wiki/City_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="City Council">City Council</a> of Northampton, placing second in a ward where the top three candidates were elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199849.E2.80.9351_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199849.E2.80.9351-16">[16]</a></sup> The position offered no salary, but provided Coolidge invaluable political experience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194083_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194083-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1899, he declined renomination, running instead for <a href="/wiki/Solicitor#United_States" title="Solicitor">City Solicitor</a>, a position elected by the City Council. He was elected for a one-year term in 1900, and reelected in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194084.E2.80.9385_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194084.E2.80.9385-19">[19]</a></sup> This position gave Coolidge more experience as a lawyer and paid a salary of $600.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess194084.E2.80.9385_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess194084.E2.80.9385-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1902, the city council selected a Democrat for city solicitor, and Coolidge returned to private practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729-20">[20]</a></sup> Soon thereafter, however, the <a href="/wiki/Court_clerk" title="Court clerk">clerk of courts</a> for the county died, and Coolidge was chosen to replace him. The position paid well, but it barred him from practicing law, so he remained at the job for only one year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1904, Coolidge suffered his sole defeat at the ballot box, losing an election to the Northampton <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Board of Education">school board</a>. When told that some of his neighbors voted against him because he had no children in the schools he would govern, Coolidge replied, "Might give me time!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy196729-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_legislator_and_mayor">State legislator and mayor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: State legislator and mayor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1906, the local Republican committee nominated Coolidge for election to the state <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_House_of_Representatives" title="Massachusetts House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>. He won a close victory over the incumbent <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>, and reported to <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> for the 1907 session of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Court" title="Massachusetts General Court">Massachusetts General Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199861_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199861-21">[21]</a></sup> In his freshman term, Coolidge served on minor committees and, although he usually voted with the party, was known as a <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive Republican</a>, voting in favor of such measures as <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seventeenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Seventeenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">direct election of Senators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199862Fuess194099_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199862Fuess194099-22">[22]</a></sup> While in Boston, Coolidge became an ally, and then a liegeman, of then U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Winthrop_Murray_Crane" class="mw-redirect" title="Winthrop Murray Crane">Winthrop Murray Crane</a> who controlled the western faction of the Massachusetts Republican Party; Crane's party rival in the east of the commonwealth was U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199863.E2.80.9366_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199863.E2.80.9366-23">[23]</a></sup> Another key strategic alliance which Coolidge forged was with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Guy_Currier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Guy Currier (page does not exist)">Guy Currier</a>, who had served in both state houses and had the social distinction, wealth, personal charm and broad circle of friends which Coolidge lacked, and which would have a lasting impact on his political career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite193899.E2.80.93102_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite193899.E2.80.93102-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1907, he was elected to a second term, and in the 1908 session, Coolidge was more outspoken, though not in a leadership position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199868.E2.80.9369_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199868.E2.80.9369-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Instead of vying for another term in the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_State_House" title="Massachusetts State House">State House</a>, Coolidge returned home to his growing family and ran for <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">mayor</a> of Northampton when the incumbent Democrat retired. He was well liked in the town, and defeated his challenger by a vote of 1,597 to 1,409.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199872_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199872-26">[26]</a></sup> During his first term (1910 to 1911), he increased teachers' salaries and retired some of the city's debt while still managing to effect a slight tax decrease.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940106.E2.80.9307Sobel199874_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940106.E2.80.9307Sobel199874-27">[27]</a></sup> He was renominated in 1911, and defeated the same opponent by a slightly larger margin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940108_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940108-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1911, the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Senate" title="Massachusetts Senate">State Senator</a> for the Hampshire County area retired and successfully encouraged Coolidge to run for his seat for the 1912 session; Coolidge defeated his Democratic opponent by a large margin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199876_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199876-29">[29]</a></sup> At the start of that term, he became chairman of a committee to arbitrate the "<a href="/wiki/Bread_and_Roses" title="Bread and Roses">Bread and Roses</a>" strike by the workers of the <a href="/wiki/American_Woolen_Company" title="American Woolen Company">American Woolen Company</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lawrence,_Massachusetts" title="Lawrence, Massachusetts">Lawrence, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[a]</a></sup> After two tense months, the company agreed to the workers' demands, in a settlement proposed by the committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940110.E2.80.9311McCoy196745.E2.80.9346_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940110.E2.80.9311McCoy196745.E2.80.9346-31">[30]</a></sup> A major issue affecting Massachusetts Republicans that year was the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1912" title="United States presidential election, 1912">party split</a> between the progressive wing, which favored <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, and the conservative wing, which favored <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>. Although he favored some progressive measures, Coolidge refused to leave the Republican party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199879.E2.80.9380Fuess1940111_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199879.E2.80.9380Fuess1940111-32">[31]</a></sup> When the new <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive Party</a> declined to run a candidate in his state senate district, Coolidge won reelection against his Democratic opponent by an increased margin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199879.E2.80.9380Fuess1940111_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199879.E2.80.9380Fuess1940111-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">Do the day's work. If it be to protect the rights of the weak, whoever objects, do it. If it be to help a powerful corporation better to serve the people, whatever the opposition, do that. Expect to be called a stand-patter, but don't be a stand-patter. Expect to be called a demagogue, but don't be a demagogue. Don't hesitate to be as revolutionary as science. Don't hesitate to be as reactionary as the multiplication table. Don't expect to build up the weak by pulling down the strong. Don't hurry to legislate. Give administration a chance to catch up with legislation.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><i><b>Have Faith in Massachusetts</b> as delivered by Calvin Coolidge to the Massachusetts State Senate, 1914.</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939-33">[32]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the 1913 session, Coolidge enjoyed renowned success in arduously navigating to passage the Western Trolley Act which connected Northampton with a dozen similar industrial communities in western Massachusetts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938105_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938105-34">[33]</a></sup> Coolidge intended to retire after his second term as was the custom, but when the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Massachusetts_Senate" title="President of the Massachusetts Senate">President of the State Senate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levi_H._Greenwood" title="Levi H. Greenwood">Levi H. Greenwood</a>, considered running for Lieutenant Governor, Coolidge decided to run again for the Senate in the hopes of being elected as its presiding officer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940114.E2.80.9315_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940114.E2.80.9315-35">[34]</a></sup> Although Greenwood later decided to run for reelection to the Senate, he was defeated primarily due to his opposition to women's suffrage; Coolidge was in favor of the women's vote, won his own re-election and with Crane's help, assumed the presidency of a closely divided Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938111_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938111-36">[35]</a></sup> After his election in January 1914, Coolidge delivered a published and frequently quoted speech entitled <i>Have Faith in Massachusetts</i>, which summarized his philosophy of government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's speech was well received, and he attracted some admirers on its account;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199890.E2.80.9392_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199890.E2.80.9392-37">[36]</a></sup> towards the end of the term, many of them were proposing his name for nomination to lieutenant governor. After winning reelection to the Senate by an increased margin in the 1914 elections, Coolidge was reelected unanimously to be President of the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199890Fuess1940124_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199890Fuess1940124-38">[37]</a></sup> Coolidge's supporters, led by fellow Amherst alumnus <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stearns" title="Frank Stearns">Frank Stearns</a>, encouraged him again to run for lieutenant governor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199892.E2.80.9398Fuess1940133.E2.80.9336_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199892.E2.80.9398Fuess1940133.E2.80.9336-39">[38]</a></sup> Stearns, an executive with the Boston department store R. H. Stearns, became another key ally, and began a publicity campaign on Coollidge's behalf before he announced his candidacy at the end of the 1915 legislative session.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938117_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938117-40">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lieutenant_Governor_and_Governor_of_Massachusetts">Lieutenant Governor and Governor of Massachusetts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lieutenant Governor and Governor of Massachusetts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CoolidgeLtGov.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CoolidgeLtGov.png/170px-CoolidgeLtGov.png" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CoolidgeLtGov.png/255px-CoolidgeLtGov.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CoolidgeLtGov.png/340px-CoolidgeLtGov.png 2x" data-file-width="797" data-file-height="1104" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CoolidgeLtGov.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calvin and Grace Coolidge, about 1918</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge entered the <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary election</a> for lieutenant governor and was nominated to run alongside gubernatorial candidate <a href="/wiki/Samuel_W._McCall" title="Samuel W. McCall">Samuel W. McCall</a>. Coolidge was the leading vote-getter in the Republican primary, and <a href="/wiki/Ticket_balance" title="Ticket balance">balanced</a> the Republican ticket by adding a western presence to McCall's eastern base of support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940139.E2.80.93142_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940139.E2.80.93142-41">[40]</a></sup> McCall and Coolidge won the 1915 election to their respective one-year terms, with Coolidge defeating his opponent by more than 50,000 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940145_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940145-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Massachusetts, the lieutenant governor does not preside over the state Senate, as is the case in many other states; nevertheless, as lieutenant governor, Coolidge was a deputy governor functioning as administrative inspector and was a member of the governor's council. He was also chairman of the finance committee and the pardons committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938125_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938125-43">[42]</a></sup> As a full-time elected official, Coolidge discontinued his law practice in 1916, though his family continued to live in Northampton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940151.E2.80.9352_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940151.E2.80.9352-44">[43]</a></sup> McCall and Coolidge were both reelected in 1916 and again in 1917. When McCall decided that he would not stand for a fourth term, Coolidge announced his intention to run for governor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998107.E2.80.93110_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998107.E2.80.93110-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1918_election">1918 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1918 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge was unopposed for the Republican nomination for <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Governors of Massachusetts">Governor</a> of Massachusetts in 1918. He and his running mate, <a href="/wiki/Channing_Cox" class="mw-redirect" title="Channing Cox">Channing Cox</a>, a Boston lawyer and Speaker of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_House_of_Representatives" title="Massachusetts House of Representatives">Massachusetts House of Representatives</a>, ran on the previous administration's record: <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">fiscal conservatism</a>, a vague opposition to <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition</a>, support for women's suffrage, and support for American involvement in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998111McCoy196775.E2.80.9376_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998111McCoy196775.E2.80.9376-46">[45]</a></sup> The issue of the war proved divisive, especially among <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish American">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Americans" title="German Americans">German Americans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998112_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998112-47">[46]</a></sup> Coolidge was elected by a margin of 16,773 votes over his opponent, Richard H. Long, in the smallest margin of victory of any of his statewide campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998115McCoy196776_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998115McCoy196776-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boston_Police_Strike">Boston Police Strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Boston Police Strike">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boston_Police_Strike" title="Boston Police Strike">Boston Police Strike</a></div>
<p>In 1919, in reaction to a plan of the policemen of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Police_Department" title="Boston Police Department">Boston Police Department</a> to register with a <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">union</a>, Police Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Edwin_U._Curtis" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin U. Curtis">Edwin U. Curtis</a> announced that such an act would not be tolerated. In August of that year, the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">American Federation of Labor</a> issued a charter to the Boston Police Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell197577.E2.80.9379Sobel1998129_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell197577.E2.80.9379Sobel1998129-49">[48]</a></sup> Curtis declared the union's leaders were guilty of insubordination and would be relieved of duty, but indicated he would cancel their suspension if the union was dissolved by September 4.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell197586.E2.80.9387_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell197586.E2.80.9387-50">[49]</a></sup> The mayor of Boston, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_James_Peters" title="Andrew James Peters">Andrew Peters</a>, convinced Curtis to delay his action for a few days, but with no results, and Curtis suspended the union leaders on September 8.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1975111.E2.80.9313Sobel1998133.E2.80.9336_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1975111.E2.80.9313Sobel1998133.E2.80.9336-51">[50]</a></sup> The following day, about three-quarters of the policemen in Boston went on strike.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1975113_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1975113-52">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[b]</a></sup> Coolidge, tacitly but fully in support of Curtis' position, closely monitored the situation but initially deferred to the local authorities. He anticipated that only a resulting measure of lawlessness could sufficiently prompt the public to understand and appreciate the controlling principle – that a policeman does not strike. That night and the next, there was sporadic violence and rioting in the unruly city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938162.E2.80.93164_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938162.E2.80.93164-54">[52]</a></sup> Peters, concerned about <a href="/wiki/Sympathy_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="Sympathy strike">sympathy strikes</a> by the firemen and others, called up some units of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_National_Guard" title="Massachusetts National Guard">Massachusetts National Guard</a> stationed in the Boston area pursuant to an old and obscure legal authority, and relieved Curtis of duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1975120_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1975120-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: left; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">"Your assertion that the Commissioner was wrong cannot justify the wrong of leaving the city unguarded. That furnished the opportunity; the criminal element furnished the action. There is no right to strike against the public safety by anyone, anywhere, any time. ... I am equally determined to defend the sovereignty of Massachusetts and to maintain the authority and jurisdiction over her public officers where it has been placed by the Constitution and laws of her people."</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><i><b>Telegram from Governor Calvin Coolidge to Samuel Gompers</b> September 14, 1919.</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1919222.E2.80.9324_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1919222.E2.80.9324-56">[54]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Coolidge, sensing the severity of circumstances were then in need of his intervention, conferred with Crane's operative, William Butler, and then acted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938164.E2.80.93165_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938164.E2.80.93165-57">[55]</a></sup> He called up more units of the National Guard, restored Curtis to office, and took personal control of the police force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998142_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998142-58">[56]</a></sup> Curtis proclaimed that all of the strikers were fired from their jobs, and Coolidge called for a new police force to be recruited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1975182.E2.80.9383_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1975182.E2.80.9383-59">[57]</a></sup> That night Coolidge received a telegram from AFL leader <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Gompers" title="Samuel Gompers">Samuel Gompers</a>. "Whatever disorder has occurred", Gompers wrote, "is due to Curtis's order in which the right of the policemen has been denied…"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998143_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998143-60">[58]</a></sup> Coolidge publicly answered Gompers's telegram, denying any justification whatsoever for the strike – and his response launched him into the national consciousness (quoted, above left).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998143_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998143-60">[58]</a></sup> Newspapers across the nation picked up on Coolidge's statement and he became the newest hero to opponents of the strike. In the midst of the <a href="/wiki/First_Red_Scare" title="First Red Scare">First Red Scare</a>, many Americans were terrified of the spread of <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communist</a> revolution, like those that had taken place in <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Hungarian Soviet Republic">Hungary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%9319" title="German Revolution of 1918–19">Germany</a>. While Coolidge had lost some friends among organized labor, conservatives across the nation had seen a rising star.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013174.E2.80.9379_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013174.E2.80.9379-61">[59]</a></sup> Although he usually acted with deliberation, the Boston police strike gave him a national reputation as a decisive leader, and as a strict enforcer of law and order.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1919_election">1919 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1919 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg/170px-Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg/255px-Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg/340px-Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_inspects_militia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge inspects militia in Boston police strike</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge and Cox were renominated for their respective offices in 1919. By this time Coolidge's supporters (especially Stearns) had publicized his actions in the Police Strike around the state and the nation and some of Coolidge's speeches were published in book form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge19192.E2.80.939-33">[32]</a></sup> He faced the same opponent as in 1918, Richard Long, but this time Coolidge defeated him by 125,101 votes, more than seven times his margin of victory from a year earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[c]</a></sup> His actions in the police strike, combined with the massive electoral victory, led to suggestions that Coolidge run for president in 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940239.E2.80.9343McCoy1967102.E2.80.9313_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940239.E2.80.9343McCoy1967102.E2.80.9313-64">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation_and_vetoes_as_governor">Legislation and vetoes as governor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legislation and vetoes as governor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the time Coolidge was inaugurated on January 2, 1919, the First World War had ended, and Coolidge pushed the legislature to give a $100 bonus to Massachusetts veterans. He also signed a bill reducing the work week for women and <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">children</a> from fifty-four hours to forty-eight, saying, "We must humanize the industry, or the system will break down."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998117Fuess1940195_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998117Fuess1940195-65">[62]</a></sup> He signed into law a budget that kept the tax rates the same, while trimming $4 million from expenditures, thus allowing the state to retire some of its debt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940186_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940186-66">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge also wielded the <a href="/wiki/Veto" title="Veto">veto</a> pen as governor. His most publicized veto prevented an increase in legislators' pay by 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940187McCoy196781_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940187McCoy196781-67">[64]</a></sup> Although Coolidge was personally opposed to Prohibition, he vetoed a bill in May 1920 that would have allowed the sale of beer or wine of <a href="/wiki/Low-alcohol_beer#Small_beer" title="Low-alcohol beer">2.75% alcohol or less</a>, in Massachusetts in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Eighteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Eighteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>. "Opinions and instructions do not outmatch the Constitution," he said in his veto message. "Against it, they are void."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940187.E2.80.9388_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940187.E2.80.9388-68">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_presidency">Vice presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vice presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1920_election">1920 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1920 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1920" title="United States presidential election, 1920">United States presidential election, 1920</a></div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1920_Republican_National_Convention" title="1920 Republican National Convention">1920 Republican National Convention</a>, most of the delegates were selected by state party conventions, not primaries. As such, the field was divided among many local favorites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998152.E2.80.9353_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998152.E2.80.9353-69">[66]</a></sup> Coolidge was one such candidate, and while he placed as high as sixth in the voting, the powerful party bosses running the convention, primarily the party's U.S. Senators, never considered him seriously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938198.E2.80.93199_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938198.E2.80.93199-70">[67]</a></sup> After ten ballots, the bosses and then the delegates settled on Senator <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> as their nominee for president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940259.E2.80.9360_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940259.E2.80.9360-71">[68]</a></sup> When the time came to select a vice presidential nominee, the bosses also made and announced their decision on whom they wanted – Sen. <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Lenroot" title="Irvine Lenroot">Irvine Lenroot</a> of Wisconsin – and then prematurely departed after his name was put forth, relying on the rank and file to confirm their decision. A delegate from <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallace_McCamant" title="Wallace McCamant">Wallace McCamant</a>, having read <i>Have Faith in Massachusetts</i>, proposed Coolidge for <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">vice president</a> instead. The suggestion caught on quickly with the masses starving for an act of independence from the absent bosses, and Coolidge was unexpectedly nominated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938211.E2.80.93213_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938211.E2.80.93213-72">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats nominated another Ohioan, <a href="/wiki/James_M._Cox" title="James M. Cox">James M. Cox</a>, for president and the Assistant Secretary of the Navy, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, for vice president. The question of the United States joining the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> was a major issue in the campaign, as was the unfinished legacy of <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">Progressivism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312-73">[70]</a></sup> Harding ran a "front-porch" campaign from his home in <a href="/wiki/Marion,_Ohio" title="Marion, Ohio">Marion, Ohio</a>, but Coolidge took to the campaign trail in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_South" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper South">Upper South</a>, New York, and New England – his audiences carefully limited to those familiar with Coolidge and those placing a premium upon concise and short speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938217.E2.80.93219_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938217.E2.80.93219-74">[71]</a></sup> On November 2, 1920, Harding and Coolidge were victorious in a landslide, winning more than 60 percent of the popular vote, including every state outside the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312-73">[70]</a></sup> They also won in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, the first time a Republican ticket had won a Southern state since <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998204.E2.80.9312-73">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Silent_Cal.22">"Silent Cal"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: &quot;Silent Cal&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HardingCoolidge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/HardingCoolidge.jpg/220px-HardingCoolidge.jpg" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/HardingCoolidge.jpg/330px-HardingCoolidge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/HardingCoolidge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="414" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HardingCoolidge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Harding and Vice President Coolidge and their wives</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The U.S. vice presidency did not carry many official duties, but Coolidge was invited by President Harding to attend cabinet meetings, making him the first vice president to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998210.E2.80.9311_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998210.E2.80.9311-75">[72]</a></sup> He gave a number of unremarkable speeches around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998219McCoy1967136_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998219McCoy1967136-76">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the U.S. vice president, Coolidge and his vivacious wife <a href="/wiki/Grace_Coolidge" title="Grace Coolidge">Grace</a> were invited to quite a few parties, where the legend of "Silent Cal" was born. It is from this time that most of the jokes and anecdotes involving Coolidge originate. Although Coolidge was known to be a skilled and effective public speaker, in private he was a man of few words and was commonly referred to as "Silent Cal". A possibly apocryphal story has it that a matron, seated next to him at a dinner, said to him, "I made a bet today that I could get more than two words out of you." He replied, "You lose."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannaford169_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannaford169-77">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Parker" title="Dorothy Parker">Dorothy Parker</a>, upon learning that Coolidge had died, reportedly remarked, "How can they tell?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg20069_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg20069-78">[75]</a></sup> Coolidge often seemed uncomfortable among fashionable Washington society; when asked why he continued to attend so many of their dinner parties, he replied, "Got to eat somewhere."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998217_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998217-79">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alice_Roosevelt_Longworth" title="Alice Roosevelt Longworth">Alice Roosevelt Longworth</a>, a leading Republican wit, underscored Coolidge's silence and his dour personality: "When he wished he were elsewhere, he pursed his lips, folded his arms, and said nothing. He looked then precisely as though he had been weaned on a pickle."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECordery2008302_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECordery2008302-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>As president, Coolidge's reputation as a quiet man continued. "The words of a President have an enormous weight," he would later write, "and ought not to be used indiscriminately."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998243_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998243-81">[78]</a></sup> Coolidge was aware of his stiff reputation; indeed, he cultivated it. "I think the American people want a solemn ass as a President," he once told <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Barrymore" title="Ethel Barrymore">Ethel Barrymore</a>, "and I think I will go along with them."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200660_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200660-82">[79]</a></sup> Some historians would later suggest that Coolidge's image was created deliberately as a campaign tactic,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuckley593.E2.80.93626_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuckley593.E2.80.93626-83">[80]</a></sup> while others believe his withdrawn and quiet behavior to be natural, deepening after the death of his son in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert87.E2.80.93109_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert87.E2.80.93109-84">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Calvin_Coolidge" title="Presidency of Calvin Coolidge">Presidency of Calvin Coolidge</a></div>
<p>On August 2, 1923, President Harding died unexpectedly in San Francisco while on a speaking tour of the western United States. Vice President Coolidge was in Vermont visiting his <a href="/wiki/Coolidge_Homestead" title="Coolidge Homestead">family home</a>, which had neither electricity nor a telephone, when he received word by messenger of Harding's death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940308.E2.80.9309_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940308.E2.80.9309-85">[82]</a></sup> He dressed, said a prayer, and came downstairs to greet the reporters who had assembled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940308.E2.80.9309_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940308.E2.80.9309-85">[82]</a></sup> His father, a <a href="/wiki/Notary_public" title="Notary public">notary public</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace#Massachusetts.2C_Connecticut.2C_Vermont" title="Justice of the peace">justice of the peace</a>, administered the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office#United_States" title="Oath of office">oath of office</a> in the family's parlor by the light of a <a href="/wiki/Kerosene_lamp" title="Kerosene lamp">kerosene lamp</a> at 2:47&#160;a.m. on August 3, 1923; President Coolidge then went back to bed.</p>
<p>Coolidge returned to Washington the next day, and was sworn in again by Justice <a href="/wiki/Adolph_A._Hoehling_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Adolph A. Hoehling Jr.">Adolph A. Hoehling Jr.</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Columbia" title="United States District Court for the District of Columbia">Supreme Court of the District of Columbia</a>, to forestall any questions about the authority of a state official to administer a federal oath.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940310.E2.80.9315_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940310.E2.80.9315-86">[83]</a></sup> This second oath taking remained a secret until it was revealed by <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._Daugherty" title="Harry M. Daugherty">Harry M. Daugherty</a> in 1932, and confirmed by Hoehling.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> When Hoehling confirmed Daugherty's story, he indicated that Daugherty, then serving as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a>, asked him to administer the oath without fanfare at the <a href="/wiki/Willard_InterContinental_Washington" title="Willard InterContinental Washington">Willard Hotel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.22Confirms_Daugherty.27s_Story_of_Coolidge.27s_Second_Oath.22_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.22Confirms_Daugherty.27s_Story_of_Coolidge.27s_Second_Oath.22-88">[85]</a></sup> According to Hoehling, he did not question Daugherty's reason for requesting a second oath taking, but assumed it was to resolve any doubt about whether the first swearing in was valid.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.22Confirms_Daugherty.27s_Story_of_Coolidge.27s_Second_Oath.22_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.22Confirms_Daugherty.27s_Story_of_Coolidge.27s_Second_Oath.22-88">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg/220px-CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg/330px-CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="338" data-file-height="234" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CalvinCoolidgeimmigration3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge signing the Immigration Act and some appropriation bills. General <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John J. Pershing</a> looks on.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The nation initially did not know what to make of Coolidge, who had maintained a low profile in the Harding administration; many had even expected him to be replaced on the ballot in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998226.E2.80.9328Fuess1940303.E2.80.9305Ferrell199843.E2.80.9351_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998226.E2.80.9328Fuess1940303.E2.80.9305Ferrell199843.E2.80.9351-89">[86]</a></sup> He appointed <a href="/wiki/C._Bascom_Slemp" title="C. Bascom Slemp">C. Bascom Slemp</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> Congressman and experienced federal politician, to work jointly with Edward T. Clark, a Massachusetts Republican organizer whom he retained from his vice-presidential staff, as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_to_the_President_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary to the President (United States)">Secretaries to the President</a> (a position equivalent to the modern <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">White House Chief of Staff</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940320.E2.80.9322_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940320.E2.80.9322-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although a few of Harding's cabinet appointees were scandal-tarred, Coolidge retained all of them, out of an ardent conviction that as successor to a deceased elected president he was obligated to retain Harding's counselors and policies until the next election. He kept Harding's able speechwriter <a href="/wiki/Judson_T._Welliver" class="mw-redirect" title="Judson T. Welliver">Judson T. Welliver</a>; Stuart Crawford replaced Welliver in November 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200648.E2.80.9349_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200648.E2.80.9349-91">[88]</a></sup> Coolidge strongly felt that those of Harding's men under suspicion were entitled to every presumption of innocence,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938265_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938265-92">[89]</a></sup> taking a characteristically methodical approach to the scandals, principally the <a href="/wiki/Teapot_Dome_scandal" title="Teapot Dome scandal">Teapot Dome scandal</a>, while others clamored for rapid punishment of those they presumed guilty. Coolidge felt the Senate investigations of the scandals would suffice; this was affirmed by the resulting resignations of those involved. He did personally intervene in demanding the resignation of Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._Daugherty" title="Harry M. Daugherty">Harry M. Daugherty</a> after he refused to cooperate with the congressional probe. He then set about to confirm that no loose ends remained in the administration, arranging for a full briefing on the wrongdoing. <a href="/wiki/Harry_A._Slattery" title="Harry A. Slattery">Harry A. Slattery</a> reviewed the facts with him, <a href="/wiki/Harlan_F._Stone" title="Harlan F. Stone">Harlan F. Stone</a> analyzed the legal aspects for him and Senator <a href="/wiki/William_E._Borah" class="mw-redirect" title="William E. Borah">William E. Borah</a> assessed and presented the political factors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938272.E2.80.93277_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938272.E2.80.93277-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge addressed Congress when it reconvened on December 6, 1923, giving a speech that supported many of Harding's policies, including Harding's formal budgeting process, the enforcement of <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1924" title="Immigration Act of 1924">immigration restrictions</a> and arbitration of coal strikes ongoing in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940328.E2.80.9329Sobel1998248.E2.80.9349_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940328.E2.80.9329Sobel1998248.E2.80.9349-94">[91]</a></sup> Coolidge's speech was the first presidential speech to be broadcast over the radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013271_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013271-95">[92]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Treaty" title="Washington Naval Treaty">Washington Naval Treaty</a> was proclaimed just one month into Coolidge's term, and was generally well received in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940320.E2.80.9322_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940320.E2.80.9322-90">[87]</a></sup> In May 1924, the World War I veterans' <a href="/wiki/World_War_Adjusted_Compensation_Act" title="World War Adjusted Compensation Act">World War Adjusted Compensation Act</a> or "Bonus Bill" was passed over his veto.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940341_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940341-96">[93]</a></sup> Coolidge signed the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1924" title="Immigration Act of 1924">Immigration Act</a> later that year, which was aimed at restricting southern and eastern European immigration, but appended a <a href="/wiki/Signing_statement" title="Signing statement">signing statement</a> expressing his unhappiness with the bill's specific exclusion of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese American">Japanese</a> immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940342Sobel1998269_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940342Sobel1998269-97">[94]</a></sup> Just before the Republican Convention began, Coolidge signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1924" title="Revenue Act of 1924">Revenue Act of 1924</a>, which reduced the top marginal tax rate from 58% to 46%, as well as personal <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a> rates across the board, increased the <a href="/wiki/Estate_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Estate tax">estate tax</a> and bolstered it with a new <a href="/wiki/Gift_tax" title="Gift tax">gift tax</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998278.E2.80.9379_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998278.E2.80.9379-98">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 2, 1924, Coolidge signed the act granting citizenship to all Native Americans born in the United States. By that time, two-thirds of the people were already citizens, having gained it through marriage, military service (veterans of World War I were granted citizenship in 1919), or the land allotments that had earlier taken place.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2-3_us_citizenship_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2-3_us_citizenship-100">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-coolidge_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coolidge-101">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1924_election">1924 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1924 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1924" title="United States presidential election, 1924">United States presidential election, 1924</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1924.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ElectoralCollege1924.svg/275px-ElectoralCollege1924.svg.png" width="275" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ElectoralCollege1924.svg/413px-ElectoralCollege1924.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ElectoralCollege1924.svg/550px-ElectoralCollege1924.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1924.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1924 electoral vote results</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1924_Republican_National_Convention" title="1924 Republican National Convention">Republican Convention</a> was held on June 10–12, 1924, in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a>; Coolidge was nominated on the first ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940345.E2.80.9346_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940345.E2.80.9346-102">[99]</a></sup> The convention nominated <a href="/wiki/Frank_Orren_Lowden" title="Frank Orren Lowden">Frank Lowden</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> for vice president on the second ballot, but he declined; former Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a> was nominated on the third ballot and accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940345.E2.80.9346_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940345.E2.80.9346-102">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats held their <a href="/wiki/1924_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1924 Democratic National Convention">convention</a> the next month in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The convention soon deadlocked, and after 103 ballots, the delegates finally agreed on a compromise candidate, <a href="/wiki/John_W._Davis" title="John W. Davis">John W. Davis</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Bryan" title="Charles W. Bryan">Charles W. Bryan</a> nominated for vice president. The Democrats' hopes were buoyed when <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_Sr." title="Robert M. La Follette Sr.">Robert M. La Follette Sr.</a>, a Republican senator from <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, split from the GOP to form a new <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1924)" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1924)">Progressive Party</a>. Many believed that the split in the Republican party, like the one in 1912, would allow a Democrat to win the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998300_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998300-103">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the conventions and the death of his younger son Calvin, Coolidge became withdrawn; he later said that "when he [the son] died, the power and glory of the Presidency went with him."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929190_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929190-104">[101]</a></sup> Even as he mourned, Coolidge ran his standard campaign, not mentioning his opponents by name or maligning them, and delivering speeches on his theory of government, including several that were broadcast over radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998300.E2.80.9301_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998300.E2.80.9301-105">[102]</a></sup> It was the most subdued campaign since 1896, partly because of Coolidge's grief, but also because of his naturally non-confrontational style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998302.E2.80.9303_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998302.E2.80.9303-106">[103]</a></sup> The other candidates campaigned in a more modern fashion, but despite the split in the Republican party, the results were similar to those of 1920. Coolidge and Dawes won every state outside the South except Wisconsin, La Follette's home state. Coolidge won the popular vote by 2.5&#160;million over his opponents' combined total.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940354_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940354-107">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_and_trade">Industry and trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Industry and trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">it is probable that a press which maintains an intimate touch with the business currents of the nation is likely to be more reliable than it would be if it were a stranger to these influences. After all, <b>the chief business of the American people is business</b>. They are profoundly concerned with buying, selling, investing and prospering in the world. (emphasis added)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><i><b>President Calvin Coolidge's address to the American Society of Newspaper Editors, Washington D.C.,</b> January 25, 1925.</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013324_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlaes2013324-108">[105]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>During Coolidge's presidency, the United States experienced a period of rapid economic growth known as the "<a href="/wiki/Roaring_Twenties" title="Roaring Twenties">Roaring Twenties</a>." He left the administration's industrial policy in the hands of his activist Secretary of Commerce, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, who energetically used government auspices to promote business efficiency and develop airlines and radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199864.E2.80.9365_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199864.E2.80.9365-109">[106]</a></sup> Coolidge disdained regulation, and demonstrated this by appointing commissioners to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a> who did little to restrict the activities of businesses under their jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199866.E2.80.9372Sobel1998318_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199866.E2.80.9372Sobel1998318-110">[107]</a></sup> The regulatory state under Coolidge was, as one biographer described it, "thin to the point of invisibility."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199872_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199872-111">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's economic policy has often been misquoted as "generally speaking, the business of the American people is business" (full quotation at right). Some have criticized Coolidge as an adherent of the <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> ideology, which they claim led to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998207_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998207-112">[109]</a></sup> On the other hand, historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sobel" title="Robert Sobel">Robert Sobel</a> offers some context based on Coolidge's sense of <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a>: "As Governor of Massachusetts, Coolidge supported wages and hours legislation, opposed <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">child labor</a>, imposed economic controls during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, favored safety measures in factories, and even worker representation on corporate boards. Did he support these measures while president? No, because in the 1920s, such matters were considered the responsibilities of state and local governments."<cite class="citation web">Sobel, Robert. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060308075125/http://www.jfklibrary.org/coolidge_sobel.html">"Coolidge and American Business"</a>. John F. Kennedy Library and Museum. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jfklibrary.org/coolidge_sobel.html">the original</a> on March 8, 2006.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACalvin+Coolidge&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Sobel&amp;rft.btitle=Coolidge+and+American+Business&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.jfklibrary.org%2Fcoolidge_sobel.html&amp;rft.pub=John+F.+Kennedy+Library+and+Museum&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200647Ferrell199862_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg200647Ferrell199862-113">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_and_government_spending">Taxation and government spending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Taxation and government spending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge's taxation policy was that of his Secretary of the Treasury, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mellon" title="Andrew Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>, the ideal that "scientific taxation"—lower taxes—actually increase rather than decrease government receipts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Greenberg2006127.E2.80.9329_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Greenberg2006127.E2.80.9329-114">[111]</a></sup> Congress agreed, and the taxes were reduced in Coolidge's term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Greenberg2006127.E2.80.9329_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Greenberg2006127.E2.80.9329-114">[111]</a></sup> In addition to these tax cuts, Coolidge proposed reductions in federal expenditures and retiring some of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_public_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="United States public debt">federal debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Fuess1940382.E2.80.9383_115-0" class="reference"><a
