href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Fuess1940382.E2.80.9383-115">[112]</a></sup> Coolidge's ideas were shared by the Republicans in Congress, and in 1924, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1924" title="Revenue Act of 1924">Revenue Act of 1924</a>, which reduced income tax rates and eliminated all income taxation for some two million people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Fuess1940382.E2.80.9383_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998310.E2.80.9311Fuess1940382.E2.80.9383-115">[112]</a></sup> They reduced taxes again by passing the Revenue Acts of <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1926" title="Revenue Act of 1926">1926</a> and <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1928" title="Revenue Act of 1928">1928</a>, all the while continuing to keep spending down so as to reduce the overall federal debt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998170_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998170-116">[113]</a></sup> By 1927, only the wealthiest 2% of taxpayers paid any federal income tax.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998170_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998170-116">[113]</a></sup> Federal spending remained flat during Coolidge's administration, allowing one-fourth of the federal debt to be retired in total. State and local governments saw considerable growth, however, surpassing the federal budget in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998174_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998174-117">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_farm_subsidies">Opposition to farm subsidies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Opposition to farm subsidies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/170px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" width="170" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/255px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/340px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="808" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge with his vice president, <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Perhaps the most contentious issue of Coolidge's presidency was relief for farmers. Some in Congress proposed a bill designed to fight falling agricultural prices by allowing the federal government to purchase crops to sell abroad at lower prices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199884McCoy1967234.E2.80.9335_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199884McCoy1967234.E2.80.9335-118">[115]</a></sup> Agriculture Secretary <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cantwell_Wallace" title="Henry Cantwell Wallace">Henry C. Wallace</a> and other administration officials favored the bill when it was introduced in 1924, but rising prices convinced many in Congress that the bill was unnecessary, and it was defeated just before the elections that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967235_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967235-119">[116]</a></sup> In 1926, with farm prices falling once more, Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_L._McNary" title="Charles L. McNary">Charles L. McNary</a> and Representative <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Haugen" title="Gilbert N. Haugen">Gilbert N. Haugen</a>—both Republicans—proposed the <a href="/wiki/McNary%E2%80%93Haugen_Farm_Relief_Bill" title="McNary–Haugen Farm Relief Bill">McNary–Haugen Farm Relief Bill</a>. The bill proposed a federal farm board that would purchase surplus production in high-yield years and hold it (when feasible) for later sale or sell it abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940383.E2.80.9384_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940383.E2.80.9384-120">[117]</a></sup> Coolidge opposed McNary-Haugen, declaring that agriculture must stand "on an independent business basis," and said that "government control cannot be divorced from political control."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940383.E2.80.9384_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940383.E2.80.9384-120">[117]</a></sup> Instead of manipulating prices, he favored instead <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>'s proposal to create profits by modernizing agriculture. Secretary Mellon wrote a letter denouncing the McNary-Haugen measure as unsound and likely to cause inflation, and it was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998327_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998327-121">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>After McNary-Haugen's defeat, Coolidge supported a less radical measure, the Curtis-Crisp Act, which would have created a federal board to lend money to farm co-operatives in times of surplus; the bill did not pass.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998327_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998327-121">[118]</a></sup> In February 1927, Congress took up the McNary-Haugen bill again, this time narrowly passing it, and Coolidge vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940388Ferrell199893_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940388Ferrell199893-122">[119]</a></sup> In his veto message, he expressed the belief that the bill would do nothing to help farmers, benefiting only exporters and expanding the federal bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998331_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998331-123">[120]</a></sup> Congress did not override the veto, but it passed the bill again in May 1928 by an increased majority; again, Coolidge vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940388Ferrell199893_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940388Ferrell199893-122">[119]</a></sup> "Farmers never have made much money," said Coolidge, the Vermont farmer's son. "I do not believe we can do much about it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199886_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199886-124">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood_control">Flood control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Flood control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge has often been criticized for his actions during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>, the worst natural disaster to hit the Gulf Coast until <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998315Barry1997286.E2.80.9387Greenberg2006132.E2.80.9335_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998315Barry1997286.E2.80.9387Greenberg2006132.E2.80.9335-125">[122]</a></sup> Although he did eventually name Secretary Hoover to a commission in charge of flood relief, scholars argue that Coolidge overall showed a lack of interest in federal flood control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998315Barry1997286.E2.80.9387Greenberg2006132.E2.80.9335_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998315Barry1997286.E2.80.9387Greenberg2006132.E2.80.9335-125">[122]</a></sup> Coolidge did not believe that personally visiting the region after the floods would accomplish anything, and that it would be seen as mere political grandstanding. He also did not want to incur the federal spending that flood control would require; he believed property owners should bear much of the cost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967330.E2.80.9331_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967330.E2.80.9331-126">[123]</a></sup> On the other hand, Congress wanted a bill that would place the federal government completely in charge of flood mitigation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarry1997372.E2.80.9374_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarry1997372.E2.80.9374-127">[124]</a></sup> When Congress passed a compromise measure in 1928, Coolidge declined to take credit for it and signed the bill in private on May 15.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006135_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006135-128">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minorities">Minorities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Minorities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/220px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/330px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/440px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3321" data-file-height="2532" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Osage_Nation" title="Osage Nation">Osage</a> men with Coolidge after he signed the bill granting Native Americans U.S. citizenship.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge was "devoid of racial prejudice," but rarely took the lead on civil rights. Coolidge disliked the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> and no Klansman is known to have received an appointment from him. In the 1924 presidential election his opponents (Robert La Follette and John Davis), and his running mate Charles Dawes, often attacked the Klan but Coolidge avoided the subject, just as he avoided most controversial topics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2014209_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014209-129">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge occasionally spoke in favor of the civil rights of <a href="/wiki/African-American" class="mw-redirect" title="African-American">African-Americans</a>, saying in his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" class="mw-redirect" title="State of the Union address">State of the Union address</a> that their rights were "just as sacred as those of any other citizen" under the U.S. Constitution and that it was a "public and a private duty to protect those rights."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998250McCoy1967328.E2.80.9329_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998250McCoy1967328.E2.80.9329-130">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1923SOTU_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1923SOTU-131">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge repeatedly called for laws to make <a href="/wiki/Lynching_in_the_United_States" title="Lynching in the United States">lynching a federal crime (it was already a state crime).</a> Congress refused to pass any such legislation. On June 2, 1924, Coolidge signed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Citizenship_Act_of_1924" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Citizenship Act of 1924">Indian Citizenship Act</a>, which granted U.S. citizenship to all American Indians living on reservations (Indians off reservations had long been citizens). <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeloria199291_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeloria199291-132">[129]</a></sup> On June 6, 1924, Coolidge delivered a commencement address at historically black, non-segregated <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">Howard University</a>, in which he thanked and commended African-Americans for their rapid advances in education and their contributions to U.S. society over the years, as well as their eagerness to render their services as soldiers in the World War, all while being faced with discrimination and prejudices at home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge192631.E2.80.9336_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge192631.E2.80.9336-133">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a speech in October 1924, Coolidge stressed tolerance of differences as an American value and thanked immigrants for their contributions to U.S. society, saying that they have "contributed much to making our country what it is." He stated that although the diversity of peoples was a detrimental source of conflict and tension in Europe, it was peculiar for the United States that it was a "harmonious" benefit for the country. Coolidge further stated the United States should assist and help immigrants who come to the country, and urged immigrants to reject "race hatreds" and "prejudices".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926159.E2.80.93156_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926159.E2.80.93156-134">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ccoolidge.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/220px-Ccoolidge.jpeg" width="220" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/330px-Ccoolidge.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/440px-Ccoolidge.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2707" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ccoolidge.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official portrait of Calvin Coolidge</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although not an isolationist, Coolidge was reluctant to enter into foreign alliances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998342_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998342-135">[132]</a></sup> He considered the 1920 Republican victory as a rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Wilsonian</a> position that the United States should join the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967184.E2.80.9385_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967184.E2.80.9385-136">[133]</a></sup> While not completely opposed to the idea, Coolidge believed the League, as then constituted, did not serve American interests, and he did not advocate membership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967184.E2.80.9385_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967184.E2.80.9385-136">[133]</a></sup> He spoke in favor of the United States joining the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a> (World Court), provided that the nation would not be bound by advisory decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967360_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967360-137">[134]</a></sup> In 1926, the Senate eventually approved joining the Court (with <a href="/wiki/Reservation_(law)" title="Reservation (law)">reservations</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967363_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967363-138">[135]</a></sup> The League of Nations accepted the reservations, but it suggested some modifications of its own.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006114.E2.80.9316_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006114.E2.80.9316-139">[136]</a></sup> The Senate failed to act; the United States never joined the World Court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006114.E2.80.9316_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006114.E2.80.9316-139">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's primary initiative was the <a href="/wiki/Kellogg%E2%80%93Briand_Pact" title="Kellogg–Briand Pact">Kellogg–Briand Pact</a> of 1928, named for Coolidge's Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Frank_B._Kellogg" title="Frank B. Kellogg">Frank B. Kellogg</a>, and French foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Briand" title="Aristide Briand">Aristide Briand</a>. The treaty, ratified in 1929, committed signatories—the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, Italy, and Japan—to "renounce war, as an instrument of national policy in their relations with one another."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940421.E2.80.9323_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940421.E2.80.9323-140">[137]</a></sup> The treaty did not achieve its intended result—the outlawry of war—but it did provide the founding principle for international law after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967380.E2.80.9381Greenberg2006123.E2.80.9324_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967380.E2.80.9381Greenberg2006123.E2.80.9324-141">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge continued the previous administration's policy of withholding recognition of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967181_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967181-142">[139]</a></sup> He also continued the United States' support for the elected government of Mexico against the <a href="/wiki/Cristero_War" title="Cristero War">rebels</a> there, lifting the arms embargo on that country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967178.E2.80.9379_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967178.E2.80.9379-143">[140]</a></sup> He sent <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Morrow" title="Dwight Morrow">Dwight Morrow</a> to Mexico as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Mexico" title="United States Ambassador to Mexico">American ambassador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998349_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998349-144">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United States' occupation of <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Nicaragua" title="United States occupation of Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Haiti" title="United States occupation of Haiti">Haiti</a> continued under his administration, but Coolidge withdrew American troops from the <a href="/wiki/1916_United_States_occupation_of_the_Dominican_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="1916 United States occupation of the Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940414.E2.80.9317Ferrell1998122.E2.80.9323_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940414.E2.80.9317Ferrell1998122.E2.80.9323-145">[142]</a></sup> Coolidge led the U.S. delegation to the <a href="/wiki/Pan-American_Conference" title="Pan-American Conference">Sixth International Conference of American States</a>, January 15–17, 1928, in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>. This was the only international trip Coolidge made during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup> There, he extended an <a href="/wiki/Olive_branch" title="Olive branch">olive branch</a> to Latin American leaders embittered over America's <a href="/wiki/Banana_Wars" title="Banana Wars">interventionist policies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup> For 88 years he was the only sitting president to have visited Cuba, until <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> did so in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1928_election">1928 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 1928 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">United States presidential election, 1928</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:480px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Calvin_Coolidge_video_montage.ogv/480px--Calvin_Coolidge_video_montage.ogv.jpg&quot; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin_Coolidge_video_montage.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Collection of video clips of President Coolidge</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the summer of 1927, Coolidge vacationed in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Hills" title="Black Hills">Black Hills</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, where he engaged in horseback riding and fly fishing and attended <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeos</a>. He made <a href="/wiki/Custer_State_Park" title="Custer State Park">Custer State Park</a> his "summer White House." While on vacation, Coolidge surprisingly issued a terse statement that he would not seek a second full term as president: "<a href="/wiki/I_do_not_choose_to_run" title="I do not choose to run">I do not choose to run for President in 1928</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998370_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998370-149">[146]</a></sup> After allowing the reporters to take that in, Coolidge elaborated. "If I take another term, I will be in the White House till 1933&#160;… Ten years in Washington is longer than any other man has had it—too long!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1938361_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1938361-150">[147]</a></sup> In his memoirs, Coolidge explained his decision not to run: "The Presidential office takes a heavy toll of those who occupy it and those who are dear to them. While we should not refuse to spend and be spent in the service of our country, it is hazardous to attempt what we feel is beyond our strength to accomplish."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929239_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929239-151">[148]</a></sup> After leaving office, he and Grace returned to Northampton, where he wrote his memoirs. The Republicans retained the White House in 1928 in the person of Coolidge's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>. Coolidge had been reluctant to endorse Hoover as his successor; on one occasion he remarked that "for six years that man has given me unsolicited advice—all of it bad."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998195_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1998195-152">[149]</a></sup> Even so, Coolidge had no desire to split the party by publicly opposing the nomination of the popular commerce secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967390.E2.80.9391Wilson1975122.E2.80.9323_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967390.E2.80.9391Wilson1975122.E2.80.9323-153">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet">Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge_judicial_appointments" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin Coolidge judicial appointments">Calvin Coolidge judicial appointments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/170px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/255px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/340px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2842" data-file-height="3606" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge appointed <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Fiske_Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Harlan Fiske Stone">Harlan Fiske Stone</a> Attorney General and then Supreme Court Justice</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge appointed one justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Fiske_Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Harlan Fiske Stone">Harlan Fiske Stone</a> in 1925. Stone was Coolidge's fellow Amherst alumnus, a Wall Street lawyer and conservative Republican. Stone was serving as dean of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a> when Coolidge appointed him to be attorney general in 1924 to restore the reputation tarnished by Harding's Attorney General, <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._Daugherty" title="Harry M. Daugherty">Harry M. Daugherty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940364_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940364-154">[151]</a></sup> Stone proved to be a firm believer in <a href="/wiki/Judicial_restraint" title="Judicial restraint">judicial restraint</a> and was regarded as one of the court's <a href="/wiki/The_Three_Musketeers_(Supreme_Court)" title="The Three Musketeers (Supreme Court)">three liberal justices</a> who would often vote to uphold New Deal legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalston.27.27passim.27.27_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalston.27.27passim.27.27-155">[152]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> later appointed Stone to be chief justice.</p>
<p>Coolidge nominated 17 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Courts of Appeals">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 61 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" class="mw-redirect" title="United States district courts">United States district courts</a>. He appointed judges to various specialty courts as well, including <a href="/wiki/Genevieve_R._Cline" title="Genevieve R. Cline">Genevieve R. Cline</a>, who became the first woman named to the federal judiciary when Coolidge placed her on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Customs_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Customs Court">United States Customs Court</a> in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman2002216_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman2002216-156">[153]</a></sup> Coolidge also signed the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Act_of_1925" title="Judiciary Act of 1925">Judiciary Act of 1925</a> into law, allowing the Supreme Court more discretion over its workload.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_and_death">Retirement and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Retirement and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_public_address.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/220px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/330px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/440px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg 2x" data-file-width="861" data-file-height="637" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_public_address.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge addressing a crowd at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>'s Roman style <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Memorial_Amphitheater" title="Arlington Memorial Amphitheater">Memorial Amphitheater</a> in 1924.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After his presidency, Coolidge retired to the modest rented house on residential Massasoit Street in <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton</a> before moving to a more spacious home, "The Beeches."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998407_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998407-157">[154]</a></sup> He kept a <a href="/wiki/Hacker-Craft" title="Hacker-Craft">Hacker</a> runabout boat on the Connecticut River and was often observed on the water by local boating enthusiasts. During this period, he also served as chairman of the non-partisan Railroad Commission, as honorary president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_the_Blind" title="American Foundation for the Blind">American Foundation for the Blind</a>, as a director of <a href="/wiki/New_York_Life_Insurance_Company" title="New York Life Insurance Company">New York Life Insurance Company</a>, as president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a>, and as a trustee of Amherst College.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940450.E2.80.9355_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940450.E2.80.9355-158">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge published <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Calvin_Coolidge" title="The Autobiography of Calvin Coolidge">his autobiography</a> in 1929 and wrote a syndicated newspaper column, "Calvin Coolidge Says," from 1930 to 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998403Ferrell1998201.E2.80.9302_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998403Ferrell1998201.E2.80.9302-159">[156]</a></sup> Faced with looming defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">1932 presidential election</a>, some Republicans spoke of rejecting Herbert Hoover as their party's nominee, and instead drafting Coolidge to run, but the former president made it clear that he was not interested in running again, and that he would publicly repudiate any effort to draft him, should it come about.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940457.E2.80.9359Greenberg2006153_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940457.E2.80.9359Greenberg2006153-160">[157]</a></sup> Hoover was renominated, and Coolidge made several radio addresses in support of him. Hoover then lost the general election to Coolidge's 1920 vice presidential Democratic opponent <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> in a landslide<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess1940460_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess1940460-161">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Death"></span> Coolidge died suddenly from <a href="/wiki/Coronary_thrombosis" title="Coronary thrombosis">coronary thrombosis</a> at "The Beeches," at 12:45&#160;p.m., January 5, 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006154.E2.80.9355_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg2006154.E2.80.9355-162">[159]</a></sup> Shortly before his death, Coolidge confided to an old friend: "I feel I no longer fit in with these times."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998410_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998410-163">[160]</a></sup> Coolidge is buried in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Notch_Cemetery" title="Plymouth Notch Cemetery">Plymouth Notch Cemetery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Notch" class="mw-redirect" title="Plymouth Notch">Plymouth Notch</a>, Vermont. The nearby family home is maintained as one of the original buildings on the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge_Homestead_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin Coolidge Homestead District">Calvin Coolidge Homestead District</a> site. The State of Vermont dedicated a new visitors' center nearby to mark Coolidge's 100th birthday on July 4, 1972.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio.2C_film.2C_and_commemorations">Radio, film, and commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Radio, film, and commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_with_press.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/170px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/255px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/340px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg 2x" data-file-width="478" data-file-height="597" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_with_press.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge, reporters, and cameramen</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his reputation as a quiet and even reclusive politician, Coolidge made use of the new medium of radio and made radio history several times while president. He made himself available to reporters, giving 520 press conferences, meeting with reporters more regularly than any president before or since.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg20067_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg20067-164">[161]</a></sup> Coolidge's <a href="/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_Calvin_Coolidge" title="Second inauguration of Calvin Coolidge">second inauguration</a> was the first presidential inauguration broadcast on radio. On December 6, 1923, he was the first president whose address to Congress was broadcast on radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel1998252_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel1998252-165">[162]</a></sup> Coolidge signed the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Act_of_1927" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Act of 1927">Radio Act of 1927</a>, which assigned regulation of radio to the newly created <a href="/wiki/Federal_Radio_Commission" title="Federal Radio Commission">Federal Radio Commission</a>. On August 11, 1924, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_W._Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodore W. Case">Theodore W. Case</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Phonofilm" title="Phonofilm">Phonofilm</a> <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-film" title="Sound-on-film">sound-on-film</a> process he developed for <a href="/wiki/Lee_DeForest" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee DeForest">Lee DeForest</a>, filmed Coolidge on the White House lawn, making Coolidge the first president to appear in a sound film. The title of the DeForest film was <i>President Coolidge, Taken on the White House Grounds</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> arrived in Washington on a U.S. Navy ship after his celebrated 1927 trans-Atlantic flight, President Coolidge welcomed him back to the U.S. and a sound-on-film record of the event exists.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:17px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/120px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg" width="120" height="116" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/180px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/240px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="259" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Coolidge was the only president to have his portrait on a coin during his lifetime, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Sesquicentennial_coinage" title="United States Sesquicentennial coinage">Sesquicentennial of American Independence Half Dollar</a>, minted in 1926.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-$5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/106px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg" width="106" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/159px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/211px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg 2x" data-file-width="511" data-file-height="580" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Coolidge on a 1938 postage stamp</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel199862Fuess194099-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel199862Fuess194099_22-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSobel1998">Sobel 1998</a>, p.&#160;62; <a href="#CITEREFFuess1940">Fuess 1940</a>, p.&#160;99.</span></li>
